<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Ice hockey</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>

<p><b>Ice hockey</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Contact_sport" title="Contact sport">contact</a> <a href="/wiki/Team_sport" title="Team sport">team sport</a> played on <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>, usually in a <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_rink" title="Ice hockey rink">rink</a>, in which two teams of <a href="/wiki/Ice_skating" title="Ice skating">skaters</a> use their <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_stick" title="Ice hockey stick">sticks</a> to <a href="/wiki/Shot_(ice_hockey)" title="Shot (ice hockey)">shoot</a> a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vulcanized" title="Vulcanized">vulcanized</a> rubber <a href="/wiki/Hockey_puck" title="Hockey puck">puck</a> into their opponent's net to score points. Ice hockey teams usually consist of six players each: one <a href="/wiki/Goaltender" title="Goaltender">goaltender</a>, and five players who skate up and down the ice trying to take the puck and score a <a href="/wiki/Goal_(ice_hockey)" title="Goal (ice hockey)">goal</a> against the opposing team.</p>
<p>A fast-paced, physical sport, ice hockey is most popular in areas of <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_in_Canada" title="Ice hockey in Canada">Canada</a> and the northern <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_in_the_United_States" title="Ice hockey in the United States">United States</a>) and northern and eastern <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. Ice hockey is the official <a href="/wiki/National_sport" title="National sport">national winter sport</a> of Canada,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> where the game enjoys immense popularity. In North America, the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a> (NHL) is the highest level for men's hockey and the most popular. The <a href="/wiki/Kontinental_Hockey_League" title="Kontinental Hockey League">Kontinental Hockey League</a> (KHL) is the highest league in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and much of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>. The <a href="/wiki/International_Ice_Hockey_Federation" title="International Ice Hockey Federation">International Ice Hockey Federation</a> (IIHF) is the formal governing body for international ice hockey. The IIHF manages international tournaments and maintains the <a href="/wiki/IIHF_World_Ranking" title="IIHF World Ranking">IIHF World Ranking</a>. Worldwide, there are ice hockey federations in 74 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ice hockey is believed to have evolved from simple stick and ball games played in the 18th and 19th century <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and elsewhere. These games were brought to North America and several similar winter games using informal rules were developed, such as "<a href="/wiki/Shinny" title="Shinny">shinny</a>" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_polo" title="Ice polo">ice polo</a>". The contemporary sport of ice hockey was developed in Canada, most notably in <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/First_indoor_hockey_game" title="First indoor hockey game">first indoor hockey game</a> was played on March 3, 1875. Some characteristics of that game, such as the length of the ice rink and the use of a puck, have been retained to this day. Amateur ice hockey leagues began in the 1880s, and <a href="/wiki/Professional_ice_hockey" title="Professional ice hockey">professional ice hockey</a> originated around 1900. The <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup" title="Stanley Cup">Stanley Cup</a>, emblematic of ice hockey club supremacy, was first awarded in 1893 to recognize the Canadian amateur champion and later became the championship trophy of the NHL. In the early 1900s, the Canadian rules were adopted by the Ligue Internationale de Hockey sur Glace, the precursor of the IIHF and the sport was played for the first time in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_at_the_Olympic_Games" title="Ice hockey at the Olympic Games">Olympics</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1920_Summer_Olympics" title="1920 Summer Olympics">Olympic Games of 1920</a>.</p>
<p>In international competitions, the national teams of six countries (The "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_Six_(hockey)" title="Big Six (hockey)">Big Six</a>") predominate: <a href="/wiki/Canada_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Canada men's national ice hockey team">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Czech Republic men's national ice hockey team">Czech Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Finland men's national ice hockey team">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Russia men's national ice hockey team">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Sweden men's national ice hockey team">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="United States men's national ice hockey team">the United States</a>. Of the 69 medals awarded all-time in men's competition at the Olympics, only six medals were not awarded to one of those countries. In the annual <a href="/wiki/Ice_Hockey_World_Championships" title="Ice Hockey World Championships">Ice Hockey World Championships</a>, 177 of 201 medals have been awarded to the six nations. Teams outside the "Big Six" have won only five medals in either competition since 1953:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> All 12 Women's Olympic and 36 <a href="/wiki/IIHF_World_Women%27s_Championships" title="IIHF World Women's Championships">IIHF World Women's Championships</a> medals have been awarded to one of these six countries, and every gold medal in both competitions has been won by either the <a href="/wiki/Canada_women%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Canada women's national ice hockey team">Canadian national team</a> or the <a href="/wiki/United_States_women%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="United States women's national ice hockey team">United States national team</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Canada, the United States, and some European countries it is known simply as "hockey"; the name "ice hockey" is used in places where "hockey" more often refers to <a href="/wiki/Field_hockey" title="Field hockey">field hockey</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_in_Africa" title="Ice hockey in Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasia</a>, and some European countries. In Russia and the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, where "hockey" can also refer to <a href="/wiki/Bandy" title="Bandy">bandy</a>, ice hockey is often called "hockey with puck".</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name"><span id="Naming"></span>Name</span></h3>
<p>The name "hockey" has no clear origin. Its first known mention is from the 1773 book <i>Juvenile Sports and Pastimes, to Which Are Prefixed, Memoirs of the Author: Including a New Mode of Infant Education</i>, by Richard Johnson (Pseud. Master Michel Angelo), whose chapter XI was titled "New Improvements on the Game of Hockey".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnTheOriginOfHockey_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-OnTheOriginOfHockey-7">[7]</a></sup> The belief that hockey was mentioned in a 1363 proclamation by <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">King Edward III of England</a> is based on modern translations of the proclamation, which was originally in Latin and explicitly forbade the games "Pilam Manualem, Pedivam, &amp; Bacularem: &amp; ad Canibucam &amp; Gallorum Pugnam". The English historian and biographer <a href="/wiki/John_Strype" title="John Strype">John Strype</a> did not use the word "hockey" when he translated the proclamation in 1720.</p>
<p>The 1573 Statute of Galway banned a sport called "'hokie'—the hurling of a little ball with sticks or staves". A form of this word was thus being used in the 16th century, though much removed from its current usage.</p>
<p>According to the Austin Hockey Association, the word "puck" derives from the Scots <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaelic_language" title="Gaelic language">Gaelic</a> <i>puc</i> or the Irish <i>poc</i> (to poke, punch or deliver a blow). "...The blow given by a hurler to the ball with his caman or hurley is always called a puck."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursors">Precursors</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hendrick_Avercamp_-_A_Scene_on_the_Ice_-_WGA01076.jpg"><img alt="Many people on the ice, doing different things." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="645" data-file-width="1044" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Hendrick_Avercamp_-_A_Scene_on_the_Ice_-_WGA01076.jpg/220px-Hendrick_Avercamp_-_A_Scene_on_the_Ice_-_WGA01076.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Hendrick_Avercamp_-_A_Scene_on_the_Ice_-_WGA01076.jpg/330px-Hendrick_Avercamp_-_A_Scene_on_the_Ice_-_WGA01076.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Hendrick_Avercamp_-_A_Scene_on_the_Ice_-_WGA01076.jpg/440px-Hendrick_Avercamp_-_A_Scene_on_the_Ice_-_WGA01076.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hendrick_Avercamp_-_A_Scene_on_the_Ice_-_WGA01076.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Winter landscape, with skaters playing Golf (<a href="/wiki/Hendrick_Avercamp" title="Hendrick Avercamp">Hendrick Avercamp</a>, 17th-century Dutch painter)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Stick-and-ball games date back to pre-Christian times. In Europe, these games included the Irish game of <a href="/wiki/Hurling" title="Hurling">hurling</a>, the closely related Scottish game of <a href="/wiki/Shinty" title="Shinty">shinty</a> and versions of <a href="/wiki/Field_hockey" title="Field hockey">field hockey</a> (including "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bandie" title="Bandie">bandie</a> ball," played in England). <a href="/wiki/IJscolf" title="IJscolf">IJscolf</a>, a game resembling <a href="/wiki/Kolven" title="Kolven">colf</a> on an ice-covered surface, was popular in the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age" title="Dutch Golden Age">Dutch Golden Age</a>. It was played with a wooden curved bat (called a <i>colf</i> or <i>kolf</i>), a wooden or leather ball and two poles (or nearby landmarks), with the objective to hit the chosen point using the least number of strokes. A similar game (<i><a href="/wiki/Knattleikr" title="Knattleikr">knattleikr</a></i>) had been played for a thousand years or more by the <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norse</a>, as documented in the <a href="/wiki/Sagas_of_Icelanders" title="Sagas of Icelanders">Icelandic sagas</a>. In England, evidence of games of "hockey on ice" (the name replaced "bandie ball"), played with a "<a href="/wiki/Bung" title="Bung">bung</a>" (a plug of cork or oak used as a stopper on a barrel) date back to the 1700s. William Pierre Le Cocq stated, in a 1799 letter written in <a href="/wiki/Chesham" title="Chesham">Chesham</a>, England:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>I must now describe to you the game of Hockey; we have each a stick turning up at the end. We get a bung. There are two sides one of them knocks one way and the other side the other way. If any one of the sides makes the bung reach that end of the churchyard it is victorious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>A 1797 engraving unearthed by Swedish sport historians Carl Gidén and Patrick Houda shows a person on <a href="/wiki/Ice_skate" title="Ice skate">skates</a> with a stick and bung on the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">River Thames</a>, probably in December 1796.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cancom_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-cancom-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>British soldiers and immigrants to Canada and the United States brought their stick-and-ball games with them and played them on the ice and snow of winter. In 1825, <a href="/wiki/John_Franklin" title="John Franklin">John Franklin</a> wrote "The game of hockey played on the ice was the morning sport" on <a href="/wiki/Great_Bear_Lake" title="Great Bear Lake">Great Bear Lake</a> during one of his <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> expeditions. A mid-1830s watercolour portrays New Brunswick lieutenant governor Archibald Campbell and his family with British soldiers on skates playing a stick-on-ice sport. Captain R.G.A. Levinge, a British Army officer in New Brunswick during Campbell's time, wrote about "hockey on ice" on Chippewa Creek (a tributary of the Niagara River) in 1839. In 1843 another British Army officer in <a href="/wiki/Kingston,_Ontario" title="Kingston, Ontario">Kingston, Ontario</a> wrote, "Began to skate this year, improved quickly and had great fun at hockey on the ice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-queens_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-queens-11">[11]</a></sup> An 1859 <i>Boston Evening Gazette</i> article referred to an early game of hockey on ice in Halifax that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-origin_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-origin-12">[12]</a></sup> An 1835 painting by John O'Toole depicts skaters with sticks and bung on a frozen stream in the American state of <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>, at that time still part of <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cancom_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-cancom-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the same era, the <a href="/wiki/Mi%27kmaq" title="Mi'kmaq">Mi'kmaq</a>, a <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a> people of <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, also had a stick-and-ball game. Canadian <a href="/wiki/Oral_history" title="Oral history">oral histories</a> describe a traditional stick-and-ball game played by the Mi'kmaq in eastern Canada, and <a href="/wiki/Silas_Tertius_Rand" title="Silas Tertius Rand">Silas Tertius Rand</a> (in his 1894 <i>Legends of the Micmacs</i>) describes a Mi'kmaq ball game known as <i>tooadijik</i>. Rand also describes a game played (probably after European contact) with <a href="/wiki/Hurley_(stick)" title="Hurley (stick)">hurleys</a>, known as <i>wolchamaadijik</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raddall_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raddall-13">[13]</a></sup> Sticks made by the Mi'kmaq were used by the British for their games.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Good_old_days_of_ice_hockey.png"><img alt="Cartoon drawing of hockey game and people falling through the ice" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="809" data-file-width="1473" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Good_old_days_of_ice_hockey.png/220px-Good_old_days_of_ice_hockey.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Good_old_days_of_ice_hockey.png/330px-Good_old_days_of_ice_hockey.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Good_old_days_of_ice_hockey.png/440px-Good_old_days_of_ice_hockey.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Good_old_days_of_ice_hockey.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Ye Gude Olde Days" from <i><a href="/wiki/Hockey:_Canada%27s_Royal_Winter_Game" title="Hockey: Canada's Royal Winter Game">Hockey: Canada's Royal Winter Game</a></i>, 1899</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Early 19th-century paintings depict <a href="/wiki/Shinney" title="Shinney">shinney</a> (or "shinny"), an early form of hockey with no standard rules which was played in Nova Scotia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Many of these early games absorbed the physical aggression of what the Mi'kmaq in Nova Scotia called <i>dehuntshigwa'es</i> (<a href="/wiki/Lacrosse" title="Lacrosse">lacrosse</a>). Shinney was played on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Lawrence_River" title="St. Lawrence River">St. Lawrence River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">Quebec City</a>, and in Kingston, Ontario<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-queens_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-queens-11">[11]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, Ontario. The number of players was often large. To this day, shinney (derived from "shinty") is a popular Canadian<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> term for <a href="/wiki/Pond_hockey" title="Pond hockey">an informal type of hockey</a>, either ice or <a href="/wiki/Street_hockey" title="Street hockey">street hockey</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Chandler_Haliburton" title="Thomas Chandler Haliburton">Thomas Chandler Haliburton</a>, in <i>The Attache: Second Series</i> (published in 1844) imagined a dialogue, between two of the novel's characters, which mentions playing "hurly on the long pond on the ice". This has been interpreted by some historians from <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Nova_Scotia" title="Windsor, Nova Scotia">Windsor, Nova Scotia</a> as reminiscence of the days when the author was a student at <a href="/wiki/King%27s-Edgehill_School" title="King's-Edgehill School">King's College School</a> in that town in 1810 and earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-origin_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-origin-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raddall_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Raddall-13">[13]</a></sup> Based on Haliburton's quote, claims were made that modern hockey was invented in Windsor, Nova Scotia, by King's College students and perhaps named after an individual ("Colonel Hockey's game").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughan199623_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughan199623-15">[15]</a></sup> Others claim that the origins of hockey come from games played in the area of Dartmouth and Halifax in Nova Scotia. However, several references have been found to hurling and shinty being played on the ice long before the earliest references from both Windsor and Dartmouth/Halifax,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-early-reference_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-early-reference-16">[16]</a></sup> and the word "hockey" was used to designate a stick-and-ball game at least as far back as 1773, as it was mentioned in the book <i>Juvenile Sports and Pastimes, to Which Are Prefixed, Memoirs of the Author: Including a New Mode of Infant Education</i> by Richard Johnson (Pseud. Master Michel Angelo), whose chapter XI was titled "New Improvements on the Game of Hockey".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_development">Initial development<span id="Foundation_of_modern_hockey"></span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_indoor_ice_rink.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="467" data-file-width="640" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Early_indoor_ice_rink.jpg/220px-Early_indoor_ice_rink.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Early_indoor_ice_rink.jpg/330px-Early_indoor_ice_rink.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Early_indoor_ice_rink.jpg/440px-Early_indoor_ice_rink.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_indoor_ice_rink.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The early Quebec Skating Rink in 1894, representative of early rinks.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:McGill_hockey_match.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of outdoor hockey game" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="471" data-file-width="608" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/McGill_hockey_match.jpg/220px-McGill_hockey_match.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/McGill_hockey_match.jpg/330px-McGill_hockey_match.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/McGill_hockey_match.jpg/440px-McGill_hockey_match.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:McGill_hockey_match.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hockey at McGill University, Montreal, 1901</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While the game's origins lie elsewhere, Montreal is at the centre of the development of the sport of contemporary ice hockey. On March 3, 1875, the <a href="/wiki/First_indoor_hockey_game" title="First indoor hockey game">first organized indoor game</a> was played at Montreal's <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Skating_Rink" title="Victoria Skating Rink">Victoria Skating Rink</a> between two nine-player teams, including <a href="/wiki/James_Creighton_(ice_hockey)" title="James Creighton (ice hockey)">James Creighton</a> and several <a href="/wiki/McGill_University" title="McGill University">McGill University</a> students. Instead of a ball or bung, the game featured a "flat circular piece of wood"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette18750303_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-gazette18750303-18">[18]</a></sup> (to keep it in the rink and to protect spectators). The goal posts were 8 feet (2.4 m) apart<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette18750303_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-gazette18750303-18">[18]</a></sup> (today's goals are six feet wide).</p>
<p>In 1876, the first game played in Montreal was reportedly "conducted under the 'Hockey Association' rules";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> the Hockey Association was England's field hockey organization. In 1877, <i>The Gazette</i> (Montreal) published a list of seven rules, six of which were largely based on six of the Hockey Association's twelve rules, with only minor differences (even the word "ball" was kept); the one added rule explained how disputes should be settled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McGill_Redmen" title="McGill Redmen">McGill University Hockey Club</a>, the first ice hockey club, was founded in 1877<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> (followed by the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Bulldogs" title="Quebec Bulldogs">Quebec Bulldogs</a> named Quebec Hockey Club and organized in 1878 and the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Victorias" title="Montreal Victorias">Montreal Victorias</a>, organized in 1881).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarrell189927_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarrell189927-22">[22]</a></sup> In 1880, the number of players per side was reduced from nine to seven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnTheOriginOfHockey_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-OnTheOriginOfHockey-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The number of teams grew, enough to hold the first "world championship" of ice hockey at Montreal's annual <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Winter_Carnival_ice_hockey_tournaments" title="Montreal Winter Carnival ice hockey tournaments">Winter Carnival</a> in 1883. The McGill team won the tournament and was awarded the "Carnival Cup."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The game was divided into thirty-minute halves. The positions were now named: <a href="/wiki/Winger_(ice_hockey)" title="Winger (ice hockey)">left and right wing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centre_(ice_hockey)" title="Centre (ice hockey)">centre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rover_(ice_hockey)" title="Rover (ice hockey)">rover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Defenceman#Historical_designations" title="Defenceman">point and cover-point</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Goaltender" title="Goaltender">goaltender</a>. In 1886, the teams competing at the Winter Carnival organized the <a href="/wiki/Amateur_Hockey_Association_of_Canada" title="Amateur Hockey Association of Canada">Amateur Hockey Association of Canada</a> (AHAC), and played a season comprising "challenges" to the existing champion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hhof_vault.jpg"><img alt="Trophy bowl and base in glass case" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Hhof_vault.jpg/220px-Hhof_vault.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Hhof_vault.jpg/330px-Hhof_vault.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Hhof_vault.jpg/440px-Hhof_vault.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hhof_vault.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The original <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup" title="Stanley Cup">Stanley Cup</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Hockey_Hall_of_Fame" title="Hockey Hall of Fame">Hockey Hall of Fame</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Europe, it is believed that in 1885 the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Ice_Hockey_Club" title="Oxford University Ice Hockey Club">Oxford University Ice Hockey Club</a> was formed to play the first <a href="/wiki/Ice_Hockey_Varsity_Match" title="Ice Hockey Varsity Match">Ice Hockey Varsity Match</a> against traditional rival <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Ice_Hockey_Club" title="Cambridge University Ice Hockey Club">Cambridge</a> in St. Moritz, Switzerland; however, this is undocumented. The match was won by the Oxford Dark Blues, 6–0;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> the first photographs and team lists date from 1895.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> This rivalry continues, claiming to be the oldest hockey rivalry in history; a similar claim is made about the rivalry between <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_University" title="Queen's University">Queen's University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Military_College_of_Canada" title="Royal Military College of Canada">Royal Military College</a> of Kingston, Ontario. Since 1986, considered the 100th anniversary of the rivalry, teams of the two colleges play for the Carr-Harris Cup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1888, the <a href="/wiki/Governor_General_of_Canada" title="Governor General of Canada">Governor General of Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Stanley,_16th_Earl_of_Derby" title="Frederick Stanley, 16th Earl of Derby">Lord Stanley of Preston</a> (whose sons and daughter were hockey enthusiasts), first attended the Montreal Winter Carnival tournament and was impressed with the game. In 1892, realizing that there was no recognition for the best team in Canada (although a number of leagues had championship trophies), he purchased a silver bowl for use as a trophy. The Dominion Hockey Challenge Cup (which later became known as the <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup" title="Stanley Cup">Stanley Cup</a>) was first awarded in <a href="/wiki/1893_AHAC_season" title="1893 AHAC season">1893</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Hockey_Club" title="Montreal Hockey Club">Montreal Hockey Club</a>, champions of the AHAC; it continues to be awarded annually to the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a>'s championship team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Stanley's son Arthur helped organize the <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Hockey_Association" title="Ontario Hockey Association">Ontario Hockey Association</a>, and Stanley's daughter <a href="/wiki/Lady_Isobel_Gathorne-Hardy" title="Lady Isobel Gathorne-Hardy">Isobel</a> was one of the first women to play ice hockey.</p>
<p>By 1893, there were almost a hundred teams in Montreal alone; in addition, there were leagues throughout Canada. <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg" title="Winnipeg">Winnipeg</a> hockey players used <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a> pads to better protect the <a href="/wiki/Goaltender" title="Goaltender">goaltender</a>'s legs; they also introduced the "scoop" shot, or what is now known as the wrist shot. <a href="/wiki/William_Fairbrother" title="William Fairbrother">William Fairbrother</a>, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontario,_Canada" title="Ontario, Canada">Ontario, Canada</a> is credited with inventing the ice hockey net in the 1890s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-standard_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-standard-30">[30]</a></sup> Goal nets became a standard feature of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Amateur_Hockey_League" title="Canadian Amateur Hockey League">Canadian Amateur Hockey League</a> (CAHL) in 1900. Left and right defence began to replace the point and cover-point positions in the OHA in 1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESelke196221_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESelke196221-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States, "ice polo", played with a ball rather than a puck, was popular during this period; however, by 1893 <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a> held their first ice hockey matches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> American financier <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Greene_Chace" title="Malcolm Greene Chace">Malcolm Greene Chace</a> is credited with being the father of hockey in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MalcolmChaseObit_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-MalcolmChaseObit-33">[33]</a></sup> In 1892, as an amateur tennis player, Chace visited <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls,_New_York" title="Niagara Falls, New York">Niagara Falls, New York</a> for a tennis match, where he met some Canadian hockey players. Soon afterwards, Chace put together a team of men from Yale, <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard</a>, and toured across Canada as captain of this team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MalcolmChaseObit_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-MalcolmChaseObit-33">[33]</a></sup> The first collegiate hockey match in the United States was played between Yale University and Johns Hopkins in Baltimore. Yale, led by captain Chace, beat Hopkins 2–1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yale_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yale-34">[34]</a></sup> In 1896, the first ice hockey league in the U.S. was formed. The U.S. Amateur Hockey League was founded in New York City, shortly after the opening of the artificial-ice <a href="/wiki/St._Nicholas_Rink" title="St. Nicholas Rink">St. Nicholas Rink</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silver7.jpg"><img alt="Team in uniform, wearing horizontally striped sweaters" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1143" data-file-width="1500" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Silver7.jpg/220px-Silver7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Silver7.jpg/330px-Silver7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Silver7.jpg/440px-Silver7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silver7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Ottawa Hockey Club "Silver Seven" (the original <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Senators_(original)" title="Ottawa Senators (original)">Ottawa Senators</a>), 1905 <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup" title="Stanley Cup">Stanley Cup</a> champions</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lord Stanley's five sons were instrumental in bringing ice hockey to Europe, defeating a court team (which included the future <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_VII_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Edward VII of the United Kingdom">Edward VII</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George V of the United Kingdom">George V</a>) at <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a> in 1895.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> By 1903, a five-team league had been founded. The <i>Ligue Internationale de Hockey sur Glace</i> was founded in 1908 to govern international competition, and the first European championship was won by Great Britain in 1910. The sport grew further in Europe in the 1920s, after ice hockey became an Olympic sport. Many <a href="/wiki/Bandy" title="Bandy">bandy</a> players switched to ice hockey so as to be able to compete in the Olympics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Converse_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Converse-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Bandy remained popular in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, which only started its ice hockey program in the 1950s. In the mid-20th century, the <i>Ligue</i> became the <a href="/wiki/International_Ice_Hockey_Federation" title="International Ice Hockey Federation">International Ice Hockey Federation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MatthewsArena2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/MatthewsArena2.jpg/220px-MatthewsArena2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/MatthewsArena2.jpg/330px-MatthewsArena2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/MatthewsArena2.jpg/440px-MatthewsArena2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MatthewsArena2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Matthews_Arena" title="Matthews Arena">Matthews Arena</a> in Boston, in use since 1910</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As the popularity of ice hockey as a spectator sport grew, earlier rinks were replaced by larger rinks. Most of the early indoor ice rinks have been demolished; Montreal's Victoria Rink, built in 1862, was demolished in 1925.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1925-sale_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-1925-sale-39">[39]</a></sup> Many older rinks succumbed to fire, such as <a href="/wiki/Denman_Arena" title="Denman Arena">Denman Arena</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dey%27s_Arena" title="Dey's Arena">Dey's Arena</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Skating_Rink" title="Quebec Skating Rink">Quebec Skating Rink</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Arena" title="Montreal Arena">Montreal Arena</a>, a hazard of the buildings' wood construction. The <a href="/wiki/Stannus_Street_Rink" title="Stannus Street Rink">Stannus Street Rink</a> in Windsor, Nova Scotia (built in 1897) may be the oldest still in existence; however, it is no longer used for ice hockey. The <a href="/wiki/Aberdeen_Pavilion" title="Aberdeen Pavilion">Aberdeen Pavilion</a> (built in 1898) in Ottawa was used for ice hockey in 1904 and is the oldest existing facility that has hosted Stanley Cup games.</p>
<p>The oldest indoor ice hockey arena still in use today for ice hockey is <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Matthews_Arena" title="Matthews Arena">Matthews Arena</a>, which was built in 1910. It has been modified extensively several times in its history and is used today by <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_University" title="Northeastern University">Northeastern University</a> for ice hockey and other sports. It was the original home rink of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Bruins" title="Boston Bruins">Boston Bruins</a> professional team,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> itself the oldest United States-based team in the NHL, starting play in the league in today's Matthews Arena on December 1, 1924. <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a> in New York City, built in 1968, is the oldest continuously-operating arena in the NHL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_era">Professional era</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ice_hockey_1922.jpg"><img alt="A young Lester Pearson waiting for a pass in an outdoor game" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="640" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ice_hockey_1922.jpg/220px-Ice_hockey_1922.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ice_hockey_1922.jpg/330px-Ice_hockey_1922.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ice_hockey_1922.jpg/440px-Ice_hockey_1922.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ice_hockey_1922.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Oxford University vs. Switzerland, 1922; future Canadian prime minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lester_Pearson" title="Lester Pearson">Lester Pearson</a> is at right front</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Professional_ice_hockey" title="Professional ice hockey">Professional ice hockey</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_National_Hockey_League" title="History of the National Hockey League">History of the National Hockey League</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Canadian_sports#Hockey" title="History of Canadian sports">History of Canadian Hockey</a></div>
<p>Professional hockey has existed since the early 20th century. By 1902, the <a href="/wiki/Western_Pennsylvania_Hockey_League" title="Western Pennsylvania Hockey League">Western Pennsylvania Hockey League</a> was the first to employ professionals. The league joined with teams in Michigan and Ontario to form the first fully professional league—the <a href="/wiki/International_Professional_Hockey_League" title="International Professional Hockey League">International Professional Hockey League</a> (IPHL)—in 1904. The WPHL and IPHL hired players from Canada; in response, Canadian leagues began to pay players (who played with amateurs). The IPHL, cut off from its largest source of players, disbanded in 1907. By then, several professional hockey leagues were operating in Canada (with leagues in Manitoba, Ontario and Quebec).</p>
<p>In 1910, the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_Association" title="National Hockey Association">National Hockey Association</a> (NHA) was formed in Montreal. The NHA would further refine the rules: dropping the rover position, dividing the game into three 20-minute periods and introducing minor and major penalties. After re-organizing as the National Hockey League (NHL) in 1917, the league expanded into the United States, starting with the Boston Bruins in 1924.</p>
<p>Professional hockey leagues developed later in Europe, but amateur leagues leading to national championships were in place. One of the first was the Swiss <a href="/wiki/National_League_A" title="National League A">National League A</a>, founded in 1916. Today, professional leagues have been introduced in most countries of Europe. Top European leagues include the <a href="/wiki/Kontinental_Hockey_League" title="Kontinental Hockey League">Kontinental Hockey League</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Extraliga" title="Czech Extraliga">Czech Extraliga</a>, the Finnish <a href="/wiki/Liiga" title="Liiga">Liiga</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Hockey_League" title="Swedish Hockey League">Swedish Hockey League</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Game">Game</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Icehockeylayout.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="348" data-file-width="748" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Icehockeylayout.svg/220px-Icehockeylayout.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Icehockeylayout.svg/330px-Icehockeylayout.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Icehockeylayout.svg/440px-Icehockeylayout.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Icehockeylayout.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Typical layout of an ice hockey rink surface</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While the general characteristics of the game stay the same wherever it is played, the exact rules depend on the particular <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_rules" title="Ice hockey rules">code of play</a> being used. The two most important codes are those of the IIHF<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and the NHL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Both of the codes, and others, originated from Canadian rules of ice hockey of the early 20th Century.</p>
<p>Ice hockey is played on a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hockey_rink" title="Hockey rink">hockey rink</a></i>. During normal play, there are six players per side on the ice at any time, one of them being the goaltender, each of whom is on ice skates. The objective of the game is to score <i><a href="/wiki/Goal_(ice_hockey)" title="Goal (ice hockey)">goals</a></i> by shooting a hard <a href="/wiki/Vulcanization" title="Vulcanization">vulcanized</a> rubber disc, the <i><a href="/wiki/Hockey_puck" title="Hockey puck">puck</a></i>, into the opponent's goal net, which is placed at the opposite end of the rink. The players use their sticks to pass or shoot the puck.</p>
<p>Within certain restrictions, players may redirect the puck with any part of their body. Players may not hold the puck in their hand and are prohibited from using their hands to pass the puck to their teammates, unless they are in the defensive zone. Players are also prohibited from kicking the puck into the opponent's goal, though intentional redirections off the skate are permitted. Players may not intentionally bat the puck into the net with their hands.</p>
<p>Hockey is an "<a href="/wiki/Offside_(ice_hockey)" title="Offside (ice hockey)">off-side</a>" game, meaning that forward passes are allowed, unlike in rugby. Before the 1930s hockey was an on-side game, meaning that only backward passes were allowed. Those rules favoured individual stick-handling as a key means of driving the puck forward. With the arrival of offside rules, the forward pass transformed hockey into a truly team sport, where individual performance diminished in importance relative to team play, which could now be coordinated over the entire surface of the ice as opposed to merely rearward players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDryden2005246_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDryden2005246-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between the six players on the ice, they are typically divided into three forwards, two defencemen, and a goaltender. The term <i>skaters</i> is typically used to describe all players who are not goaltenders. The <i><a href="/wiki/Forward_(ice_hockey)" title="Forward (ice hockey)">forward</a></i> positions consist of a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_(ice_hockey)" title="Center (ice hockey)">centre</a></i> and two <i><a href="/wiki/Winger_(ice_hockey)" title="Winger (ice hockey)">wingers</a></i>: a <i>left wing</i> and a <i>right wing</i>. Forwards often play together as units or <i>lines</i>, with the same three forwards always playing together. The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Defenceman_(ice_hockey)" title="Defenceman (ice hockey)">defencemen</a></i> usually stay together as a pair generally divided between left and right. Left and right side wingers or defencemen are generally positioned as such, based on the side on which they carry their stick. A substitution of an entire unit at once is called a <i>line change</i>. Teams typically employ alternate sets of forward lines and defensive pairings when <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shorthanded" title="Shorthanded">shorthanded</a></i> or on a <i><a href="/wiki/Power_play_(sporting_term)" title="Power play (sporting term)">power play</a></i>. The goaltender stands in a, usually blue, semi-circle called the <i>crease</i> in the defensive zone keeping pucks from going in. Substitutions are permitted at any time during the game, although during a stoppage of play the home team is permitted the final change. When players are substituted during play, it is called changing <i>on the fly</i>. A new NHL rule added in the 2005–2006 season prevents a team from changing their line after they <i><a href="/wiki/Icing_(ice_hockey)" title="Icing (ice hockey)">ice</a></i> the puck.</p>
<p>The boards surrounding the ice help keep the puck in play and they can also be used as tools to play the puck. Players are permitted to "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bodycheck" title="Bodycheck">bodycheck</a>" opponents into the boards as a means of stopping progress. The referees, linesmen and the outsides of the goal are "in play" and do not cause a stoppage of the game when the puck or players are influenced (by either bouncing or colliding) into them. Play can be stopped if the goal is knocked out of position. Play often proceeds for minutes without interruption. When play is stopped, it is restarted with a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faceoff" title="Faceoff">faceoff</a>". Two players "face" each other and an official drops the puck to the ice, where the two players attempt to gain control of the puck. Markings on the ice indicate the locations for the faceoff and guide the positioning of players.</p>
<p>The three major rules of play in ice hockey that limit the movement of the puck: "offside", "icing", and the puck going out of play. A player is "offside" if he enters his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hockey_rink#Zones" title="Hockey rink">opponent's zone</a> before the puck itself. Under many situations, a player may not "ice the puck", shoot the puck all the way across both the centre line and the opponent's goal line. The puck goes "out of play" whenever it goes past the perimeter of the ice rink (onto the player benches, over the "glass," or onto the protective netting above the glass) and a stoppage of play is called by the officials using whistles. It also does not matter if the puck comes back onto the ice surface from those areas as the puck is considered dead once it leaves the perimeter of the rink.</p>
<p>Under IIHF rules, each team may carry a maximum of 20 players and two goaltenders on their roster. NHL rules restrict the total number of players per game to 18, plus two goaltenders. In the NHL, the players are usually divided into four lines of three forwards, and into three pairs of defencemen. On occasion, teams may elect to substitute an extra defenceman for a forward; this seventh defenceman might sometimes play on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourth_line" title="Fourth line">fourth line</a> as a forward.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="see Talk page (January 2016)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Periods_and_overtime">Periods and overtime</span></h3>
<p>A professional game consists of three <i>periods</i> of twenty minutes, the clock running only when the puck is in play. The teams change ends after each period of play, including overtime. Recreational leagues and children's leagues often play shorter games, generally with three shorter periods of play.</p>
<p>Various procedures are used if a game is tied. In tournament play, as well as in the NHL playoffs, North Americans favour <i>sudden death <a href="/wiki/Overtime_(ice_hockey)" title="Overtime (ice hockey)">overtime</a></i>, in which the teams continue to play twenty-minute periods until a goal is scored. Up until the 1999–2000 season regular season NHL games were settled with a single five-minute sudden death period with five players (plus a goalie) per side, with both teams awarded one point in the standings in the event of a tie. With a goal, the winning team would be awarded two points and the losing team none (just as if they had lost in regulation).</p>
<p>From 1999–2000 until 2003–04, the National Hockey League decided ties by playing a single five-minute sudden death overtime period with each team having four skaters per side (plus the goalie) to "open up" the game. In the event of a tie, each team would still receive one point in the standings but in the event of a victory the winning team would be awarded two points in the standings and the losing team one point. The idea was to discourage teams from playing for a tie, since previously some teams might have preferred a tie and 1 point to risking a loss and zero points. The only exception to this rule is if a team opts to pull their goalie in exchange for an extra skater during overtime and is subsequently scored upon (an "empty net" goal), in which case the losing team receives no points for the overtime loss. Since the 2015-16 season, the single five-minute sudden death overtime session involves three skaters on each side. Since three skaters must always be on the ice in an NHL game, the consequences of penalties are slightly different from those during regulation play. If a team is on a powerplay when overtime begins, that team will play with more than three skaters (usually four, very rarely five) until the expiration of the penalty. Any penalty during overtime that would result in a team losing a skater during regulation instead causes the non-penalized team to add a skater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>International play and several North American professional leagues, including the NHL (in the regular season), now use an overtime period identical to that from 99–00 – 03–04 followed by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shootout_(hockey)" title="Shootout (hockey)">penalty shootout</a>. If the score remains tied after an extra overtime period, the subsequent shootout consists of three players from each team taking penalty shots. After these six total shots, the team with the most goals is awarded the victory. If the score is still tied, the shootout then proceeds to a <i><a href="/wiki/Sudden_death_(sport)" title="Sudden death (sport)">sudden death</a></i> format. Regardless of the number of goals scored during the shootout by either team, the final score recorded will award the winning team one more goal than the score at the end of regulation time. In the NHL if a game is decided in overtime or by a shootout the winning team is awarded two points in the standings and the losing team is awarded one point. Ties no longer occur in the NHL.</p>
<p>The overtime mode for the NHL playoffs differ from the regular season. In the playoffs there are no shootouts nor ties. If a game is tied after regulation an additional 20 minutes of 5 on 5 sudden death overtime will be added. In case of a tied game after the overtime, multiple 20-minute overtimes will be played until a team scores, which wins them the match.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Penalties">Penalties</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Penalty_(ice_hockey)" title="Penalty (ice hockey)">Penalty (ice hockey)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hockeyfight.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="711" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Hockeyfight.JPG/170px-Hockeyfight.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Hockeyfight.JPG/255px-Hockeyfight.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Hockeyfight.JPG/340px-Hockeyfight.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hockeyfight.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Altercations often occur near the goal after a stoppage of play, since defensive players are highly concerned with protecting their <a href="/wiki/Goaltender" title="Goaltender">goaltender</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In ice hockey, infractions of the rules lead to play stoppages whereby the play is restarted at a face off. Some infractions result in the imposition of a <i>penalty</i> to a player or team. In the simplest case, the offending player is sent to the "<a href="/wiki/Penalty_box" title="Penalty box">penalty box</a>" and their team has to play with one fewer player on the ice for a designated amount of time. <i>Minor</i> penalties last for two minutes, <i>major</i> penalties last for five minutes, and a <i>double minor</i> penalty is two <i>consecutive</i> penalties of two minutes duration. A single minor penalty may be extended by a further two minutes for causing visible injury to the victimized player. This is usually when blood is drawn during high sticking. Players may be also assessed personal extended penalties or game expulsions for misconduct in addition to the penalty or penalties their team must serve. The team that has been given a penalty is said to be playing "short-handed" while the opposing team is on a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_play_(sport)" title="Power play (sport)">power play</a>."</p>
<p>A two-minute minor penalty is often charged for lesser infractions such as "<a href="/wiki/Tripping_(ice_hockey)" title="Tripping (ice hockey)">tripping</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Elbow_(strike)" title="Elbow (strike)">elbowing</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Roughing" title="Roughing">roughing</a>", "<a href="/wiki/High-sticking" title="High-sticking">high-sticking</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Delay_of_game" title="Delay of game">delay of the game</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Too_many_men" title="Too many men">too many players on the ice</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Boarding_(ice_hockey)" title="Boarding (ice hockey)">boarding</a>", illegal equipment, "<a href="/wiki/Charging_(ice_hockey)" title="Charging (ice hockey)">charging</a>" (leaping into an opponent or body-checking him after taking more than two strides), "holding", holding the stick (grabbing an opponent's stick), "interference", "<a href="/wiki/Hooking_(ice_hockey)" title="Hooking (ice hockey)">hooking</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Slashing_(ice_hockey)" title="Slashing (ice hockey)">slashing</a>", "kneeing", "unsportsmanlike conduct" (arguing a penalty call with referee, extremely vulgar or inappropriate verbal comments), "butt-ending" (striking an opponent with the knob of the stick—a very rare penalty), "spearing", or "<a href="/wiki/Cross-checking" title="Cross-checking">cross-checking</a>". As of the 2005–2006 season, a minor penalty is also assessed for "<a href="/wiki/Diving_(ice_hockey)" title="Diving (ice hockey)">diving</a>", where a player embellishes or simulates an offence. More egregious fouls may be penalized by a four-minute double-minor penalty, particularly those that injure the victimized player. These penalties end either when the time runs out or when the other team scores during the power play. In the case of a goal scored during the first two minutes of a double-minor, the penalty clock is set down to two minutes upon a score, effectively expiring the first minor penalty. Five-minute major penalties are called for especially violent instances of most minor infractions that result in intentional injury to an opponent, or when a "minor" penalty results in visible injury (such as bleeding), as well as for fighting. Major penalties are always served in full; they do not terminate on a goal scored by the other team. Major penalties assessed for fighting are typically offsetting, meaning neither team is short-handed and the players exit the penalty box upon a stoppage of play following the expiration of their respective penalties. The foul of "boarding" (defined as "check[ing] an opponent in such a manner that causes the opponent to be thrown violently in the boards")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> is penalized either by a minor or major penalty at the discretion of the referee, based on the violent state of the hit. A minor or major penalty for boarding is often assessed when a player checks an opponent from behind and into the boards.</p>
<p>Some varieties of penalties do not always require the offending team to play a man short. Concurrent five-minute major penalties in the NHL usually result from fighting. In the case of two players being assessed five-minute fighting majors, both the players serve five minutes without their team incurring a loss of player (both teams still have a full complement of players on the ice). This differs with two players from opposing sides getting minor penalties, at the same time or at any intersecting moment, resulting from more common infractions. In this case, both teams will have only four skating players (not counting the goaltender) until one or both penalties expire (if one penalty expires before the other, the opposing team gets a power play for the remainder of the time); this applies regardless of current pending penalties. However, in the NHL, a team always has at least three skaters on the ice. Thus, ten-minute <i>misconduct</i> penalties are served in full by the penalized player, but his team may immediately substitute another player on the ice <i>unless</i> a minor or major penalty is assessed in conjunction with the misconduct (a <i>two-and-ten</i> or <i>five-and-ten</i>). In this case, the team designates another player to serve the minor or major; both players go to the penalty box, but only the designee may not be replaced, and he is released upon the expiration of the two or five minutes, at which point the ten-minute misconduct begins. In addition, <i>game misconducts</i> are assessed for deliberate intent to inflict severe injury on an opponent (at the officials' discretion), or for a major penalty for a stick infraction or repeated major penalties. The offending player is ejected from the game and must immediately leave the playing surface (he does not sit in the penalty box); meanwhile, if an additional minor or major penalty is assessed, a designated player must serve out of that segment of the penalty in the box (similar to the above-mentioned "two-and-ten"). In some rare cases, a player may receive up to nineteen minutes in penalties for one string of plays. This could involve receiving a four-minute double minor penalty, getting in a fight with an opposing player who retaliates, and then receiving a game misconduct after the fight. In this case, the player is ejected and two teammates must serve the double-minor and major penalties.</p>
<p>A "<a href="/wiki/Penalty_shot_(ice_hockey)" title="Penalty shot (ice hockey)">penalty shot</a>" is awarded to a player when the illegal actions of another player stop a clear scoring opportunity, most commonly when the player is on a "<a href="/wiki/Breakaway_(ice_hockey)" title="Breakaway (ice hockey)">breakaway</a>". A penalty shot allows the obstructed player to pick up the puck on the centre red-line and attempt to score on the goalie with no other players on the ice, to compensate for the earlier missed scoring opportunity. A penalty shot is also awarded for a defender other than the goaltender covering the puck in the goal crease, a goaltender intentionally displacing his own goal posts during a breakaway to avoid a goal, a defender intentionally displacing his own goal posts when there is less than two minutes to play in regulation time or at any point during overtime, or a player or coach intentionally throwing a stick or other object at the puck or the puck carrier and the throwing action disrupts a shot or pass play.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Referee_hockey_ahl_2004.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="545" data-file-width="439" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Referee_hockey_ahl_2004.jpg/170px-Referee_hockey_ahl_2004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Referee_hockey_ahl_2004.jpg/255px-Referee_hockey_ahl_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Referee_hockey_ahl_2004.jpg/340px-Referee_hockey_ahl_2004.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Referee_hockey_ahl_2004.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An ice hockey referee is responsible for assessing most penalties during a game.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Officials also stop play for puck movement violations, such as using one's hands to pass the puck in the offensive end, but no players are penalized for these offences. The sole exceptions are deliberately falling on or gathering the puck to the body, carrying the puck in the hand, and shooting the puck out of play in one's defensive zone (all penalized two minutes for delay of game).</p>
<p>In the NHL, a unique penalty applies to the goalies. The goalies now are forbidden to play the puck in the "corners" of the rink near their own net. This will result in a two-minute penalty against the goalie's team. Only in the area in-front of the goal line and immediately behind the net (marked by two red lines on either side of the net) the goalie can play the puck.</p>
<p>An additional rule that has never been a penalty, but was an infraction in the NHL before recent rules changes, is the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two-line_pass" title="Two-line pass">two-line offside pass</a>." Prior to the 2005–06 NHL season, play was stopped when a pass from inside a team's defending zone crossed the centre line, with a face-off held in the defending zone of the offending team. Now, the centre line is no longer used in the NHL to determine a two-line pass infraction, a change that the IIHF had adopted in 1998. Players are now able to pass to teammates who are more than the blue and centre ice red line away.</p>
<p>The NHL has taken steps to speed up the game of hockey and create a game of finesse, by retreating from the past where illegal hits, fights, and "clutching and grabbing" among players were commonplace. Rules are now more strictly enforced, resulting in more penalties, which in turn provides more protection to the players and facilitates more goals being scored. The governing body for United States amateur hockey has implemented many new rules to reduce the number of stick-on-body occurrences, as well as other detrimental and illegal facets of the game ("zero tolerance").</p>
<p>In men's hockey, but not in women's, a player may use his hip or shoulder to hit another player if the player has the puck or is the last to have touched it. This use of the hip and shoulder is called "<a href="/wiki/Checking_(ice_hockey)" title="Checking (ice hockey)">body checking</a>." Not all physical contact is legal—in particular, hits from behind, hits to the head and most types of forceful stick-on-body contact are illegal.</p>
<p>A <i>delayed penalty call</i> occurs when a penalty offence is committed by the team that does not have possession of the puck. In this circumstance the team with possession of the puck is allowed to complete the play; that is, play continues until a goal is scored, a player on the opposing team gains control of the puck, or the team in possession commits an infraction or penalty of their own. Because the team on which the penalty was called cannot control the puck without stopping play, it is impossible for them to score a goal. In these cases the team in possession of the puck can pull the goalie for an extra attacker without fear of being scored on. However, it is possible for the controlling team to mishandle the puck into their own net. If a delayed penalty is signalled and the team in possession scores, the penalty is still assessed to the offending player, but not served. In 2012, this rule was changed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NCAA" title="NCAA">NCAA</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/College_hockey" title="College hockey">college level hockey</a> in the United States. In college games, the penalty is still enforced even if the team in possession scores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Officials">Officials</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Official_(ice_hockey)" title="Official (ice hockey)">Official (ice hockey)</a></div>
<p>A typical game of hockey is governed by two to four <i><a href="/wiki/Official_(ice_hockey)" title="Official (ice hockey)">officials</a></i> on the ice, charged with enforcing the rules of the game. There are typically two <i>linesmen</i> who are mainly responsible for calling "offside" and "<a href="/wiki/Icing_(ice_hockey)" title="Icing (ice hockey)">icing</a>" violations, breaking up fights, and conducting faceoffs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> and one or two <i>referees</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> who call goals and all other penalties. Linesmen can, however, report to the referee(s) that a penalty should be assessed against an offending player in some situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The restrictions on this practice vary depending on the governing rules. On-ice officials are assisted by off-ice officials who act as goal judges, time keepers, and official scorers.</p>
<p>The most widespread system in use today is the "three-man system," that uses one referee and two linesmen. Another less commonly used system is the two referee and one linesman system. This system is very close to the regular three-man system except for a few procedure changes. With the first being the National Hockey League, a number of leagues have started to implement the "four-official system," where an additional referee is added to aid in the calling of penalties normally difficult to assess by one single referee. The system is now used in every NHL game, at <a href="/wiki/Ice_Hockey_World_Championships" title="Ice Hockey World Championships">IIHF World Championships</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olympics" title="Olympics">Olympics</a> and in many professional and high-level amateur leagues in North America and Europe.</p>
<p>Officials are selected by the league they work for. Amateur hockey leagues use guidelines established by national organizing bodies as a basis for choosing their officiating staffs. In North America, the national organizing bodies <a href="/wiki/Hockey_Canada" title="Hockey Canada">Hockey Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/USA_Hockey" title="USA Hockey">USA Hockey</a> approve officials according to their experience level as well as their ability to pass rules knowledge and skating ability tests. Hockey Canada has officiating levels I through VI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> USA Hockey has officiating levels 1 through 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment">Equipment</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wallinheimo_Sinuhe_JYP.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2345" data-file-width="1637" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Wallinheimo_Sinuhe_JYP.jpg/220px-Wallinheimo_Sinuhe_JYP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Wallinheimo_Sinuhe_JYP.jpg/330px-Wallinheimo_Sinuhe_JYP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Wallinheimo_Sinuhe_JYP.jpg/440px-Wallinheimo_Sinuhe_JYP.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wallinheimo_Sinuhe_JYP.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Goaltenders use more protective equipment than other players.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_equipment" title="Ice hockey equipment">Ice hockey equipment</a></div>
<p>Since ice hockey is a full contact sport in men's hockey, body checks are allowed so injuries are a common occurrence. Protective equipment is mandatory and is enforced in all competitive situations. This includes a helmet (cage worn if certain age or clear plastic visor can be worn), shoulder pads, elbow pads, mouth guard, protective gloves, heavily padded shorts (also known as hockey pants) or a girdle, athletic cup (also known as a jock, for males; and jill, for females), shin pads, skates, and (optionally) a neck protector.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_goaltending_equipment" title="Ice hockey goaltending equipment">Goaltenders</a> use different equipment. With <a href="/wiki/Hockey_puck" title="Hockey puck">hockey pucks</a> approaching them at speeds of up to 100 mph (160 km/h) they must wear equipment with more protection. Goaltenders wear specialty goalie skates (these skates are built more for movement side to side rather than forwards and backwards), a jock or jill, large leg pads (there are size restrictions in certain leagues), blocking glove, catching glove, a chest protector, a goalie mask, and a large jersey. Goaltenders' equipment has continually become larger and larger, leading to fewer goals in each game and many official rule changes.</p>
<p>Ice hockey skates are optimized for physical acceleration, speed and maneuverability. This includes rapid starts, stops, turns, and changes in skating direction. In addition, they must be rigid and tough to protect the skater's feet from contact with other skaters, sticks, pucks, the boards, and the ice itself. Rigidity also improves the overall maneuverability of the skate. Blade length, thickness (width), and curvature (rocker/radius (front to back) and radius of hollow (across the blade width) are quite different from speed or figure skates. Hockey players usually adjust these parameters based on their skill level, position, and body type. Most skate's width are about an 1/8 of an inch (3 mm) thick.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_stick" title="Ice hockey stick">ice hockey stick</a> consists of a long, relatively wide, and slightly curved flat blade, attached to a shaft. The curve itself has a big impact on its performance. A deep curve allows for lifting the puck easier while a shallow curve allows for easier backhand shots. The flex of the stick also impacts the performance. Typically, a less flexible stick is meant for a stronger player since the player is looking for the right balanced flex that allows the stick to flex easily while still having a strong "whip-back" which sends the puck flying at high speeds. It is quite distinct from sticks in other sports games and most suited to hitting and controlling the flat puck. Its unique shape contributed to the early development of the game.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Injury">Injury</span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Cleanup" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This article may <b>require <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup">cleanup</a></b> to meet Wikipedia's <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style">quality standards</a>. The specific problem is: "Both sections are closely related" <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ice_hockey&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> if you can.</span> <small><i>(January 2016)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Ice hockey is a full contact sport and carries a high risk of injury. Players are moving at speeds around approximately 20–30 mph (30–50 km/h) and quite a bit of the game revolves around the physical contact between the players. Skate blades, hockey sticks, shoulders, hips, and hockey pucks all contribute. The types of injuries associated with hockey include: lacerations, concussions, contusions, ligament tears, broken bones, hyperextensions, and muscle strains. Women's ice hockey players can have contact but are not allowed to body check.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Head_injuries">Head injuries</span></h3>
<p>According to the Hughston Health Alert, "Lacerations to the head, scalp, and face are the most frequent types of injury [in hockey]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hughston.com_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-hughston.com-53">[53]</a></sup> Even a shallow cut to the head results in a loss of a large amount of blood. Not only are lacerations common, "it is estimated that direct trauma accounts for 80% of all [hockey] injuries. Most of these injuries are caused by player contact, falls and contact with a puck, high stick and occasionally, a skate blade."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hughston.com_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-hughston.com-53">[53]</a></sup> One of the causes of head injury is checking from behind. Due to the danger of delivering a check from behind, many leagues, including the NHL have made this a major and game misconduct penalty (called "boarding"). Another type of check that accounts for many of the player-to-player contact concussions is a check to the head resulting in a misconduct penalty (called "head contact"). A check to the head can be defined as delivering a hit while the receiving player's head is down and their waist is bent and the aggressor is targeting the opponent player's head. The most dangerous result of a head injury in hockey can be classified as a concussion. Most concussions occur during player-to-player contact rather than when a player is checked into the boards. Checks to the head have accounted for nearly 50% of concussions that players in the National Hockey League have suffered. Concussions that players suffer may go unreported because there is no obvious physical signs if a player is not knocked unconscious. This can prove to be dangerous if a player decides to return to play without receiving proper medical attention. Studies show that, ice hockey causes 44.3% of all traumatic brain injuries among Canadian children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In severe cases, the traumatic brain injuries are capable of resulting in death. Occurrences of death from these injuries are rare, but occur all too much in a variety of sports.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tactics">Tactics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OHL-Hockey-Plymouth-Whalers-vs-Saginaw-Spirit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="840" data-file-width="1262" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/OHL-Hockey-Plymouth-Whalers-vs-Saginaw-Spirit.jpg/220px-OHL-Hockey-Plymouth-Whalers-vs-Saginaw-Spirit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/OHL-Hockey-Plymouth-Whalers-vs-Saginaw-Spirit.jpg/330px-OHL-Hockey-Plymouth-Whalers-vs-Saginaw-Spirit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/OHL-Hockey-Plymouth-Whalers-vs-Saginaw-Spirit.jpg/440px-OHL-Hockey-Plymouth-Whalers-vs-Saginaw-Spirit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OHL-Hockey-Plymouth-Whalers-vs-Saginaw-Spirit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Winning the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faceoff" title="Faceoff">faceoff</a> can be the key to some strategies. A game between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saginaw" title="Saginaw">Saginaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plymouth,_Michigan" title="Plymouth, Michigan">Plymouth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Hockey_League" title="Ontario Hockey League">Ontario Hockey League</a> teams.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Checking">Checking</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Checking_(ice_hockey)" title="Checking (ice hockey)">Checking (ice hockey)</a></div>
<p>An important defensive tactic is checking—attempting to take the puck from an opponent or to remove the opponent from play. <i>Stick checking</i>, <i>sweep checking</i>, and <i>poke checking</i> are legal uses of the stick to obtain possession of the puck. The <i><a href="/wiki/Neutral_zone_trap" title="Neutral zone trap">neutral zone trap</a></i> is designed to isolate the puck carrier in the neutral zone preventing him from entering the offensive zone. <i>Body checking</i> is using one's shoulder or hip to strike an opponent who has the puck or who is the last to have touched it (the last person to have touched the puck is still legally "in possession" of it, although a penalty is generally called if he is checked more than two seconds after his last touch). Often the term checking is used to refer to body checking, with its true definition generally only propagated among fans of the game.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Offensive_tactics">Offensive tactics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
