<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Peter_Bondra_scoring.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1024" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Peter_Bondra_scoring.jpg/220px-Peter_Bondra_scoring.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Peter_Bondra_scoring.jpg/330px-Peter_Bondra_scoring.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Peter_Bondra_scoring.jpg/440px-Peter_Bondra_scoring.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Peter_Bondra_scoring.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Peter_Bondra" title="Peter Bondra">Peter Bondra</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Thrashers" title="Atlanta Thrashers">Atlanta Thrashers</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shot_(hockey)" title="Shot (hockey)">shoots the puck</a> and scores against <a href="/wiki/Roberto_Luongo" title="Roberto Luongo">Roberto Luongo</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Panthers" title="Florida Panthers">Florida Panthers</a> during the <a href="/wiki/2005%E2%80%9306_NHL_season" title="2005–06 NHL season">2005–06 NHL season</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Shot_(ice_hockey)" title="Shot (ice hockey)">Shot (ice hockey)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slapshot" title="Slapshot">Slapshot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wrist_shot" title="Wrist shot">Wrist shot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snap_shot_(ice_hockey)" title="Snap shot (ice hockey)">Snap shot (ice hockey)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Backhand_slapshot" title="Backhand slapshot">Backhand slapshot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Offside_(ice_hockey)" title="Offside (ice hockey)">Offside (ice hockey)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extra_attacker" title="Extra attacker">Extra attacker</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Deke_(ice_hockey)" title="Deke (ice hockey)">Deke (ice hockey)</a></div>
<p>Offensive tactics include improving a team's position on the ice by advancing the puck out of one's zone towards the opponent's zone, progressively by gaining lines, first your own blue line, then the red line and finally the opponent's blue line. <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League_rules" title="National Hockey League rules">NHL rules</a> instated for the 2006 season redefined the offside rule to make the two-line pass legal; a player may pass the puck from behind his own blue line, past both that blue line and the centre red line, to a player on the near side of the opponents' blue line. Offensive tactics are designed ultimately to score a goal by taking a shot. When a player purposely directs the puck towards the opponent's goal, he or she is said to "shoot" the puck.</p>
<p>A <i>deflection</i> is a shot that redirects a shot or a pass towards the goal from another player, by allowing the puck to strike the stick and carom towards the goal. A <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One-timer" title="One-timer">one-timer</a></i> is a shot struck directly off a pass, without receiving the pass and shooting in two separate actions. <i>Headmanning the puck</i>, also known as <i>breaking out</i>, is the tactic of rapidly passing to the player farthest down the ice. <i><a href="/wiki/Loafing_(ice_hockey)" title="Loafing (ice hockey)">Loafing</a></i>, also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Cherry_picking_(basketball)" title="Cherry picking (basketball)">cherry-picking</a></i>, is when a player, usually a forward, skates behind an attacking team, instead of playing defence, in an attempt to create an easy scoring chance.</p>
<p>A team that is losing by one or two goals in the last few minutes of play will often elect to <i>pull the goalie</i>; that is, remove the goaltender and replace him or her with an <i><a href="/wiki/Extra_attacker" title="Extra attacker">extra attacker</a></i> on the ice in the hope of gaining enough advantage to score a goal. However, it is an act of desperation, as it sometimes leads to the opposing team extending their lead by scoring a goal in the empty net.</p>
<p>One of the most important strategies for a team is their <i><a href="/wiki/Forecheck" title="Forecheck">forecheck</a></i>. Forechecking is the act of attacking the opposition in their defensive zone. Forechecking is an important part of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dump_and_chase" title="Dump and chase">dump and chase</a></i> strategy (i.e. shooting the puck into the offensive zone and then chasing after it). Each team will use their own unique system but the main ones are: <a href="/wiki/2-1-2_Forecheck" title="2-1-2 Forecheck">2–1–2</a>, 1–2–2, and 1–4. The 2–1–2 is the most basic forecheck system where two forwards will go in deep and pressure the opposition's defencemen, the third forward stays high and the two defencemen stay at the blueline. The 1–2–2 is a bit more conservative system where one forward pressures the puck carrier and the other two forwards cover the oppositions' wingers, with the two defencemen staying at the blueline. The 1–4 is the most defensive forecheck system, referred to as the neutral zone trap, where one forward will apply pressure to the puck carrier around the oppositions' blueline and the other 4 players stand basically in a line by their blueline in hopes the opposition will skate into one of them. Another strategy is the <a href="/wiki/Left_wing_lock" title="Left wing lock">left wing lock</a>, which has two forwards pressure the puck and the left wing and the two defencemen stay at the blueline.</p>
<p>There are many other little tactics used in the game of hockey. <i><a href="/wiki/Cycling_(ice_hockey)" title="Cycling (ice hockey)">Cycling</a></i> moves the puck along the boards in the offensive zone to create a <a href="/wiki/Scoring_chance" title="Scoring chance">scoring chance</a> by making defenders tired or moving them out of position. <i>Pinching</i> is when a defenceman pressures the opposition's winger in the offensive zone when they are breaking out, attempting to stop their attack and keep the puck in the offensive zone. A <i><a href="/wiki/Saucer_pass" title="Saucer pass">saucer pass</a></i> is a pass used when an opposition's stick or body is in the passing lane. It is the act of raising the puck over the obstruction and having it land on a teammate's stick.</p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Deke_(ice_hockey)" title="Deke (ice hockey)">deke</a>, short for "decoy," is a <a href="/wiki/Feint" title="Feint">feint</a> with the body or stick to fool a defender or the goalie. Many modern players, such as <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Datsyuk" title="Pavel Datsyuk">Pavel Datsyuk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Crosby" title="Sidney Crosby">Sidney Crosby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Kane" title="Patrick Kane">Patrick Kane</a>, have picked up the skill of "dangling," which is fancier deking and requires more stick handling skills.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fights">Fights</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fighting_in_ice_hockey" title="Fighting in ice hockey">Fighting in ice hockey</a></div>
<p>Although fighting is officially prohibited in the rules, it is both a target of criticism and a considerable draw for the sport. At the professional level in North America fights are unofficially condoned. <a href="/wiki/Enforcer_(ice_hockey)" title="Enforcer (ice hockey)">Enforcers</a> and other players fight to demoralize the opposing players while exciting their own, as well as settling personal scores. A fight will also break out if one of the team's skilled players gets hit hard or someone gets hit by what the team perceives as a dirty hit. The amateur game penalizes fisticuffs more harshly, as a player who receives a fighting major is also assessed at least a 10-minute misconduct penalty (NCAA and some Junior leagues) or a game misconduct penalty and <a href="/wiki/Suspension_(punishment)" title="Suspension (punishment)">suspension</a> (high school and younger, as well as some casual adult leagues).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Crowds seem to like fighting in ice hockey and cheer when fighting erupts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Women.27s_ice_hockey">Women's ice hockey</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Dream_of_the_Future_(Women%27s_Ice_Hockey).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1819" data-file-width="1068" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/A_Dream_of_the_Future_%28Women%27s_Ice_Hockey%29.png/170px-A_Dream_of_the_Future_%28Women%27s_Ice_Hockey%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/A_Dream_of_the_Future_%28Women%27s_Ice_Hockey%29.png/255px-A_Dream_of_the_Future_%28Women%27s_Ice_Hockey%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/A_Dream_of_the_Future_%28Women%27s_Ice_Hockey%29.png/340px-A_Dream_of_the_Future_%28Women%27s_Ice_Hockey%29.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Dream_of_the_Future_(Women%27s_Ice_Hockey).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Drawing of a female ice hockey player, from <i><a href="/wiki/Hockey:_Canada%27s_Royal_Winter_Game" title="Hockey: Canada's Royal Winter Game">Hockey: Canada's Royal Winter Game</a></i> (1899)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ice hockey is one of the fastest growing women's sports in the world, with the number of participants increasing by 400 percent in the 10 years through 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In 2011, Canada had 85,827 women players,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> United States had 65,609,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Finland 4,760,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Sweden 3,075<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and Switzerland 1,172.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> While there are not as many organized leagues for women as there are for men, there exist leagues of all levels, including the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Women%27s_Hockey_League" title="Canadian Women's Hockey League">Canadian Women's Hockey League</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Women%27s_Hockey_League" title="Western Women's Hockey League">Western Women's Hockey League</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Women%27s_Hockey_League_(2015%E2%80%93)" title="National Women's Hockey League (2015–)">National Women's Hockey League</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Women%27s_Hockey_League" title="Mid-Atlantic Women's Hockey League">Mid-Atlantic Women's Hockey League</a>, and various European leagues; as well as university teams, national and Olympic teams, and <a href="/wiki/Recreation" title="Recreation">recreational</a> teams. The IIHF holds a <a href="/wiki/IIHF_World_Women%27s_Championships" title="IIHF World Women's Championships">IIHF World Women's Championship</a> tournament annually except in Olympic years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IIHF_WWC_list_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-IIHF_WWC_list-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Girls_ice_hockey_team_1921.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="760" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Girls_ice_hockey_team_1921.jpg/220px-Girls_ice_hockey_team_1921.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Girls_ice_hockey_team_1921.jpg/330px-Girls_ice_hockey_team_1921.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Girls_ice_hockey_team_1921.jpg/440px-Girls_ice_hockey_team_1921.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Girls_ice_hockey_team_1921.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A women's ice hockey team in 1921</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The chief difference between women's and men's hockey is that body checking is not allowed in women's hockey. After the 1990 Women's World Championship, body checking was eliminated in women's hockey. In current IIHF women's competition, body checking is either a minor or major <a href="/wiki/Penalty_(ice_hockey)" title="Penalty (ice hockey)">penalty</a>, decided at the referee's discretion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IIHF_Rules_Section_6_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-IIHF_Rules_Section_6-64">[64]</a></sup> In addition, players in women's competition are required to wear protective full-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hockey_mask" title="Hockey mask">face masks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IIHF_Rules_Section_6_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-IIHF_Rules_Section_6-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Canada, to some extent <a href="/wiki/Ringette" title="Ringette">ringette</a> serves as the female counterpart to ice hockey, in the sense that in many families, the boys play hockey while the girls play ringette.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Canadian_women%27s_ice_hockey_history" title="Canadian women's ice hockey history">Canadian women's ice hockey history</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_women%27s_ice_hockey_in_the_United_States" title="History of women's ice hockey in the United States">History of women's ice hockey in the United States</a></div>
<p>Women are known to have played the game in the 19th century. Several games were recorded in the 1890s in <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, Ontario, Canada. The women of Lord Stanley's family were known to participate in the game of ice hockey on the outdoor ice rink at <a href="/wiki/Rideau_Hall" title="Rideau Hall">Rideau Hall</a>, the residence of Canada's Governor-General.</p>
<p>The game developed at first without an organizing body. A tournament in 1902 between Montreal and Trois-Rivieres was billed as the first championship tournament. Several tournaments, such as at the Banff Winter Carnival, were held in the early 20th Century and numerous women's teams such as the Seattle Vamps and Vancouver Amazons existed. Organizations started to develop in the 1920s, such as the Ladies Ontario Hockey Association, and later, the Dominion Women's Amateur Hockey Association. Starting in the 1960s, the game spread to universities. Today, the game is played from youth through adult leagues, and in the universities of North America and internationally. There are two major women's hockey leagues, the <a href="/wiki/National_Women%27s_Hockey_League_(2015%E2%80%93)" title="National Women's Hockey League (2015–)">National Women's Hockey League</a> with teams in the Northeastern United States which is a professional league and the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Women%27s_Hockey_League" title="Canadian Women's Hockey League">Canadian Women's Hockey League</a> with teams in Canada and the United States, which is semi-professional and is developing toward becoming a fully professional league.</p>
<p>The first women's world championship tournament, albeit unofficial, was held in 1987 in <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>, Ontario, Canada. This was followed by the first IIHF World Championship in 1990 in Ottawa. Women's ice hockey was added as a medal sport at the <a href="/wiki/1998_Winter_Olympics" title="1998 Winter Olympics">1998 Winter Olympics</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nagano,_Nagano" title="Nagano, Nagano">Nagano, Japan</a>. The United States won the gold, Canada won the silver and Finland won the bronze medal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunter_1998_Olympics_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hunter_1998_Olympics-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Hockey_League" title="United States Hockey League">United States Hockey League</a> (USHL) welcomed the first female professional hockey player in 1969–70, when the Marquette Iron Rangers signed <a href="/wiki/Karen_Koch" title="Karen Koch">Karen Koch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> One woman, <a href="/wiki/Manon_Rh%C3%A9aume" title="Manon Rhéaume">Manon Rhéaume</a>, has played in the NHL, as a goaltender for the <a href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay_Lightning" title="Tampa Bay Lightning">Tampa Bay Lightning</a> in pre-season games against the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Blues" title="St. Louis Blues">St. Louis Blues</a> and the Boston Bruins. In 2003, <a href="/wiki/Hayley_Wickenheiser" title="Hayley Wickenheiser">Hayley Wickenheiser</a> played with the <a href="/wiki/Kirkkonummi" title="Kirkkonummi">Kirkkonummi</a> <a href="/wiki/HC_Salamat" title="HC Salamat">Salamat</a> in the Finnish men's <a href="/wiki/Suomi-sarja" title="Suomi-sarja">Suomi-sarja</a> league. Several women have competed in North American minor leagues, including Rhéaume, goaltenders <a href="/wiki/Kelly_Dyer" title="Kelly Dyer">Kelly Dyer</a> and Erin Whitten and defenceman <a href="/wiki/Angela_Ruggiero" title="Angela Ruggiero">Angela Ruggiero</a>.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Hockey_League_.28NHL.29">National Hockey League (NHL)</span></h4>
<p>The NHL is by far the best attended and most popular ice hockey league in the world. The league's history began after Canada's <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_Association" title="National Hockey Association">National Hockey Association</a> decided to disband in 1917; the result was the creation of the National Hockey League. The league expanded to the United States beginning in 1924. In 1967, the NHL doubled in size to 12 teams, undertaking one of the greatest expansions in professional sports history. A few years later, in 1972, a new 12 team league, the <a href="/wiki/World_Hockey_Association" title="World Hockey Association">World Hockey Association</a> (WHA) was formed and due to its ensuing rivalry with the NHL, it caused an escalation in players salaries. As of 1979, the NHL had grown to 17 teams and merged with the WHA. This created a 21 team league.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-70">[70]</a></sup> By 1999, the NHL had expanded to 30 teams, and after a realignment in 2013, these teams were divided into two conferences and four divisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_leagues">Minor leagues</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Hockey_League" title="American Hockey League">American Hockey League</a> (AHL), sometimes referred to as "The A,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> is the primary developmental professional league for players aspiring to enter the NHL. It comprises 30 teams from the United States and Canada. It is run as a "farm league" to the NHL, with the vast majority of AHL players under contract to an NHL team. The <a href="/wiki/ECHL" title="ECHL">ECHL</a> (called the East Coast Hockey League before the 2003–04 season) is a mid-level minor league in the United States with a few players under contract to NHL or AHL teams. The <a href="/wiki/Southern_Professional_Hockey_League" title="Southern Professional Hockey League">Southern Professional Hockey League</a> (SPHL) is a developmental minor league in the United States with no NHL affiliations. Most undrafted players get their start in the ECHL or SPHL.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="University_competitions">University competitions</span></h4>
<p>In the United States especially, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/College_hockey" title="College hockey">college hockey</a> is popular and the best university teams compete in the annual <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Men%27s_Ice_Hockey_Championship" title="NCAA Men's Ice Hockey Championship">NCAA Men's Ice Hockey Championship</a>. The <a href="/wiki/American_Collegiate_Hockey_Association" title="American Collegiate Hockey Association">American Collegiate Hockey Association</a> is composed of college teams at the club level.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Junior_leagues">Junior leagues</span></h4>
<p>In Canada, the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Hockey_League" title="Canadian Hockey League">Canadian Hockey League</a> is an umbrella organization comprising three major junior leagues: the <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Hockey_League" title="Ontario Hockey League">Ontario Hockey League</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hockey_League" title="Western Hockey League">Western Hockey League</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Major_Junior_Hockey_League" title="Quebec Major Junior Hockey League">Quebec Major Junior Hockey League</a>. It attracts players from Canada, the United States and Europe. In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Hockey_League" title="United States Hockey League">United States Hockey League</a> (USHL) is the highest junior league. Players in this league are strictly amateur, so that they may play college hockey if they wish.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eurasia">Eurasia</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HC_LEV_Record_O2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1224" data-file-width="1632" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/HC_LEV_Record_O2.jpg/220px-HC_LEV_Record_O2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/HC_LEV_Record_O2.jpg/330px-HC_LEV_Record_O2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/HC_LEV_Record_O2.jpg/440px-HC_LEV_Record_O2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HC_LEV_Record_O2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Kontinental_Hockey_League" title="Kontinental Hockey League">KHL</a> match</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kontinental_Hockey_League_.28KHL.29">Kontinental Hockey League (KHL)</span></h4>
<p>The Kontinental Hockey League (KHL) is the largest and most popular ice hockey league in <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>. The league is the direct successor to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Super_League" title="Russian Super League">Russian Super League</a>, which in turn was the successor to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Championship_League" title="Soviet Championship League">Soviet League</a>, the history of which dates back to the Soviet adoption of ice hockey in the 1940s. The KHL was <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%9309_KHL_season" title="2008–09 KHL season">launched in 2008</a> with clubs predominantly from Russia, but featuring teams from other <a href="/wiki/Post-Soviet_states" title="Post-Soviet states">post-Soviet states</a>. The league expanded beyond the former Soviet countries beginning in the <a href="/wiki/2011%E2%80%9312_KHL_season" title="2011–12 KHL season">2011–12 season</a>, with clubs in Croatia and Slovakia. The number of teams has since increased to 28 from eight different countries.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_leagues_2">Minor leagues</span></h4>
<p>The second division of hockey in Eurasia is the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Hockey_League" title="Supreme Hockey League">Supreme Hockey League</a> (VHL). This league features 24 teams from Russia and 2 from Kazakhstan. This league is currently being converted to a farm league for the KHL, similarly to the AHL's function in relation to the NHL. The third division is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Hockey_League" title="Russian Hockey League">Russian Hockey League</a>, which features only teams from Russia. The <a href="/wiki/Asia_League_Ice_Hockey" title="Asia League Ice Hockey">Asia League</a>, an international ice hockey league featuring clubs from China, Japan, South Korea, and the Russian Far East, is the successor to the <a href="/wiki/Japan_Ice_Hockey_League" title="Japan Ice Hockey League">Japan Ice Hockey League</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Junior_leagues_2">Junior leagues</span></h4>
<p>The highest junior league in Eurasia is the <a href="/wiki/Junior_Hockey_League_(Russia)" title="Junior Hockey League (Russia)">Junior Hockey League</a> (MHL). It features 32 teams from post-Soviet states, predominantly Russia. The second tier to this league is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Junior_Hockey_League_Championships" title="Junior Hockey League Championships">Junior Hockey League Championships</a> (MHL-B).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h3>
<p>Several countries in Europe have their own top professional senior leagues. Many future KHL and NHL players start or end their professional careers in these leagues. The <a href="/wiki/National_League_A" title="National League A">National League A</a> in Switzerland, <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Hockey_League" title="Swedish Hockey League">Swedish Hockey League</a> in Sweden, <a href="/wiki/Liiga" title="Liiga">Liiga</a> in Finland, and <a href="/wiki/Czech_Extraliga" title="Czech Extraliga">Czech Extraliga</a> in the Czech Republic are all very popular in their respective countries.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Champions_Hockey_League">Champions Hockey League</span></h4>
<p>Beginning in the 2014–15 season, the <a href="/wiki/Champions_Hockey_League" title="Champions Hockey League">Champions Hockey League</a> was launched, a league consisting of first-tier teams from several European countries, running parallel to the teams' domestic leagues. The competition is meant to serve as a Europe-wide ice hockey club championship. The competition is a direct successor to the <a href="/wiki/European_Trophy" title="European Trophy">European Trophy</a> and is related to the 2008–09 <a href="/wiki/Champions_Hockey_League_(2008%E2%80%9309)" title="Champions Hockey League (2008–09)">tournament of the same name</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h4>
<p>There are also several annual tournaments for clubs, held outside of league play. <a href="/wiki/Season_(sports)" title="Season (sports)">Pre-season</a> tournaments include the <a href="/wiki/European_Trophy" title="European Trophy">European Trophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tampere_Cup" title="Tampere Cup">Tampere Cup</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pajulahti_Cup" title="Pajulahti Cup">Pajulahti Cup</a>. One of the oldest international ice hockey competition for clubs is the <a href="/wiki/Spengler_Cup" title="Spengler Cup">Spengler Cup</a>, held every year in <a href="/wiki/Davos" title="Davos">Davos</a>, Switzerland, between Christmas and New Year's Day. It was first awarded in 1923 to the Oxford University Ice Hockey Club. The <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Cup" title="Memorial Cup">Memorial Cup</a>, a competition for junior-level (age 20 and under) clubs is held annually from a pool of junior championship teams in Canada and the United States.</p>
<p>International club competitions organized by the IIHF include the <a href="/wiki/IIHF_Continental_Cup" title="IIHF Continental Cup">Continental Cup</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cup_(ice_hockey)" title="Victoria Cup (ice hockey)">Victoria Cup</a> and the European Women's Champions Cup. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Junior_Club_Cup" title="World Junior Club Cup">World Junior Club Cup</a> is an annual tournament of <a href="/wiki/Junior_ice_hockey" title="Junior ice hockey">junior ice hockey</a> clubs representing each of the top junior leagues.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_team_competitions">National team competitions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Finlande_Russie.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="527" data-file-width="722" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Finlande_Russie.jpg/220px-Finlande_Russie.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Finlande_Russie.jpg/330px-Finlande_Russie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Finlande_Russie.jpg/440px-Finlande_Russie.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Finlande_Russie.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Finland vs Russia in the <a href="/wiki/2006_Winter_Olympics" title="2006 Winter Olympics">Winter Olympics 2006 in Turin</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_at_the_Olympic_Games" title="Ice hockey at the Olympic Games">Ice hockey</a> has been played at the <a href="/wiki/Winter_Olympic_Games" title="Winter Olympic Games">Winter Olympics</a> since 1924 (and was played at the <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_at_the_1920_Summer_Olympics" title="Ice hockey at the 1920 Summer Olympics">summer games in 1920</a>). Canada won six of the first seven <a href="/wiki/Gold_medal" title="Gold medal">gold medals</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_at_the_1952_Winter_Olympics" title="Ice hockey at the 1952 Winter Olympics">1952</a>, the exception occurring in <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_at_the_1936_Winter_Olympics" title="Ice hockey at the 1936 Winter Olympics">1936</a> when Great Britain won. The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">USSR</a> won all but two gold medals from <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_at_the_1956_Winter_Olympics" title="Ice hockey at the 1956 Winter Olympics">1956</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_at_the_1988_Winter_Olympics" title="Ice hockey at the 1988 Winter Olympics">1988</a> as well as a final time as the <a href="/wiki/Unified_Team_at_the_Olympics" title="Unified Team at the Olympics">Unified Team</a> at the <a href="/wiki/1992_Winter_Olympics" title="1992 Winter Olympics">1992 Albertville Olympics</a>. The United States won their first gold medal in <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_at_the_1960_Winter_Olympics" title="Ice hockey at the 1960 Winter Olympics">1960</a>. On the way to winning the gold medal at the <a href="/wiki/1980_Winter_Olympics" title="1980 Winter Olympics">1980 Lake Placid Olympics</a> amateur US college players defeated the heavily favoured Soviet squad—an event known as the "<a href="/wiki/Miracle_on_Ice" title="Miracle on Ice">Miracle on Ice</a>" in the United States. Restrictions on professional players were fully dropped at the 1998 games in Nagano. The Games saw the full participation of players from the NHL, which suspended operations during the Games and has done so in subsequent Games. The <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">2010 games in Vancouver</a> were the first played in an NHL city since the inclusion of NHL players. The 2010 games were the first played on NHL-sized ice rinks, which are narrower than the IIHF standard.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eroeffnungsspiel_eishockey_wm_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Eroeffnungsspiel_eishockey_wm_2010.jpg/220px-Eroeffnungsspiel_eishockey_wm_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Eroeffnungsspiel_eishockey_wm_2010.jpg/330px-Eroeffnungsspiel_eishockey_wm_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Eroeffnungsspiel_eishockey_wm_2010.jpg/440px-Eroeffnungsspiel_eishockey_wm_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eroeffnungsspiel_eishockey_wm_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/2010_Men%27s_World_Ice_Hockey_Championships" title="2010 Men's World Ice Hockey Championships">Ice Hockey World Championships 2010</a> in Germany</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>National teams representing the member federations of the IIHF compete annually in the IIHF Ice Hockey World Championships. Teams are selected from the available players by the individual federations, without restriction on amateur or professional status. Since it is held in the spring, the tournament coincides with the annual NHL <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="Stanley Cup playoffs">Stanley Cup playoffs</a> and many of the top players are hence not available to participate in the tournament. Many of the NHL players who do play in the IIHF tournament come from teams eliminated before the playoffs or in the first round, and federations often hold open spots until the tournament to allow for players to join the tournament after their club team is eliminated. For many years, the tournament was an amateur-only tournament, but this restriction was removed, beginning in the 1970s. Players are not paid to play in the tournament, but insurance and expenses are covered from the tournament revenues.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Summit_Series" title="Summit Series">1972 Summit Series</a> and <a href="/wiki/1974_Summit_Series" title="1974 Summit Series">1974 Summit Series</a>, two series pitting the best Canadian and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet" title="Soviet">Soviet</a> players without IIHF restrictions were major successes, and established a rivalry between Canada and the USSR. In the spirit of best-versus-best without restrictions on amateur or professional status, the series were followed by five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canada_Cup_(ice_hockey)" title="Canada Cup (ice hockey)">Canada Cup</a> tournaments, played in North America. Two NHL versus USSR series were also held: the 1979 Challenge Cup and <a href="/wiki/Rendez-vous_%2787" title="Rendez-vous '87">Rendez-vous '87</a>. The Canada Cup tournament later became the <a href="/wiki/World_Cup_of_Hockey" title="World Cup of Hockey">World Cup of Hockey</a>, played in 1996 and 2004. The United States won in 1996 and Canada won in 2004.</p>
<p>Since the initial women's world championships in 1990, there have been fifteen tournaments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IIHF_WWC_list_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-IIHF_WWC_list-63">[63]</a></sup> Women's hockey has been played at the Olympics since 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunter_1998_Olympics_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hunter_1998_Olympics-65">[65]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_at_the_2006_Winter_Olympics" title="Ice hockey at the 2006 Winter Olympics">2006 Winter Olympic final</a> between Canada and Sweden marked the only time the women's world championship or Olympic final did not involve both Canada and the United States.</p>
<p>Other ice hockey tournaments featuring national teams include the <a href="/wiki/IIHF_World_U20_Championship" title="IIHF World U20 Championship">World U20 Championship</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IIHF_World_U18_Championships" title="IIHF World U18 Championships">World U18 Championships</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_U-17_Hockey_Challenge" title="World U-17 Hockey Challenge">World U-17 Hockey Challenge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Junior_A_Challenge" title="World Junior A Challenge">World Junior A Challenge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Hlinka_Memorial_Tournament" title="Ivan Hlinka Memorial Tournament">Ivan Hlinka Memorial Tournament</a>, the <a href="/wiki/IIHF_World_Women%27s_U18_Championships" title="IIHF World Women's U18 Championships">World Women's U18 Championships</a> and the <a href="/wiki/4_Nations_Cup" title="4 Nations Cup">4 Nations Cup</a>. The annual <a href="/wiki/Euro_Hockey_Tour" title="Euro Hockey Tour">Euro Hockey Tour</a>, an unofficial European championship between the national men's teams of the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Czech Republic men's national ice hockey team">Czech Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Finland men's national ice hockey team">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Russia men's national ice hockey team">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sweden_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Sweden men's national ice hockey team">Sweden</a> have been played since 1996–97.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Miscellaneous">Miscellaneous</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Australian_Ice_Hockey_League" title="Australian Ice Hockey League">Australian Ice Hockey League</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Ice_Hockey_League" title="New Zealand Ice Hockey League">New Zealand Ice Hockey League</a> are represented by nine and five teams respectively. As of 2012, the two top teams of the previous season from each league compete in the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Tasman_Champions_League" title="Trans-Tasman Champions League">Trans-Tasman Champions League</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_in_Africa" title="Ice hockey in Africa">Ice hockey in Africa</a> is a small but growing sport so no African ice hockey playing nation has a domestic league.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pond_hockey">Pond hockey</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pond_hockey" title="Pond hockey">Pond hockey</a></div>
<p>Pond hockey is a form of ice hockey played generally as pick-up hockey on lakes, ponds and artificial outdoor rinks during the winter. Pond hockey is commonly referred to in hockey circles as shinny. Its rules differ from traditional hockey because there is no hitting and very little shooting, placing a greater emphasis on skating, puckhandling and passing abilities. Since 2002, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Pond_Hockey_Championship" title="World Pond Hockey Championship">World Pond Hockey Championship</a> has been played on Roulston Lake in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plaster_Rock" title="Plaster Rock">Plaster Rock</a>, New Brunswick, Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Since 2006, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Pond_Hockey_Championships" title="U.S. Pond Hockey Championships">U.S. Pond Hockey Championships</a> have been played in Minneapolis, Minnesota, and the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_National_Pond_Hockey_Championships" title="Canadian National Pond Hockey Championships">Canadian National Pond Hockey Championships</a> have been played in Huntsville, Ontario.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_in_popular_culture" title="Ice hockey in popular culture">Ice hockey in popular culture</a></div>
<p>Ice hockey is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sports_in_Canada#Official_sports" title="Sports in Canada">official winter sport of Canada</a>. Ice hockey, partially because of its popularity as a major professional sport, has been a source of inspiration for numerous films, television episodes and songs in North American popular culture.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Attendance_records">Attendance records</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_ice_hockey_games_with_highest_attendance" title="List of ice hockey games with highest attendance">List of ice hockey games with highest attendance</a></div>
<p>The record for a Stanley Cup playoff game is 28,183, set on April 23, 1996, at the <a href="/wiki/Tropicana_Field" title="Tropicana Field">Thunderdome</a> during a Tampa Bay Lightning – <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Flyers" title="Philadelphia Flyers">Philadelphia Flyers</a> game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>A record was set on December 11, 2010, when the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Wolverines_men%27s_ice_hockey" title="Michigan Wolverines men's ice hockey">men's ice hockey team</a> faced cross-state rival <a href="/wiki/Michigan_State_Spartans_men%27s_ice_hockey" title="Michigan State Spartans men's ice hockey">Michigan State</a> in an event billed as "<a href="/wiki/The_Big_Chill_at_the_Big_House" title="The Big Chill at the Big House">The Big Chill at the Big House</a>." The game was played at <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Wolverines_football" title="Michigan Wolverines football">Michigan's (American) football</a> venue, <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Stadium" title="Michigan Stadium">Michigan Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ann_Arbor,_Michigan" title="Ann Arbor, Michigan">Ann Arbor</a>, with a capacity of 109,901 as of the <a href="/wiki/2010_Michigan_Wolverines_football_team" title="2010 Michigan Wolverines football team">2010 football season</a>. When UM stopped sales to the public on May 6, 2010, with plans to reserve remaining tickets for students, over 100,000 tickets had been sold for the event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Ultimately, a crowd announced by UM as 113,411, the largest in the stadium's history (including football), saw the homestanding Wolverines win 5–0. <i><a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a></i>, using a count of ticketed fans who actually entered the stadium instead of UM's figure of tickets sold, announced a final figure of 104,173.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janela_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janela-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Big_Chill_record_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Big_Chill_record-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The record was approached but not broken at the <a href="/wiki/2014_NHL_Winter_Classic" title="2014 NHL Winter Classic">2014 NHL Winter Classic</a>, which also held at Michigan Stadium, with the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Red_Wings" title="Detroit Red Wings">Detroit Red Wings</a> as the home team and the <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Maple_Leafs" title="Toronto Maple Leafs">Toronto Maple Leafs</a> as the opposing team with an announced crowd of 105,491.</p>
